But the wounds that are made by scarification should be wounds that are placed on the liver, and on the spleen and gout, and on the navel, in the places of the joints, and on the tip of the belly button, because these members do not tolerate scarification on themselves.
And it is not desired for those [wounds], but rather the attraction of blood from the member, just as we place a leech on the nipple in a disease of blood flow from the nose; and we use that so that the cold, viscous air from the member may be dissolved, just as we place it on the belly and navel; because that rarefies the member and warms it and removes the pain because it resolves the air.
And it is already placed on the boils when they occur in their opening or the pus, because by virtue of the strong attraction, the pus is drawn out and the boil is eradicated from its place.
And similarly it does when it is placed on the liver and spleen, drawing out the air that is enclosed in those places.
And these wounds are administered empty with suction alone; and sometimes they are administered with fire.

Thus begins the prologue of the third part: of restoration. This chapter is of great necessity in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations that occur in the bones.
Oh, son, know that these foolish doctors and common folk boast about this chapter, and those who never at any time regarded the books of the Ancients nor read even a single letter from them.
And for this reason it is made that this manner of the learned in our land is deprived; and you have not forgotten this at any time well; and I have not adopted this that I have learned, but for the length of reading the books of the Ancients and study over the understanding of those, until that I extracted the science from those.
And therefore I turn to experiment and study in the length of my life.

First Chapter.
Summaries and collections from the being of fractures of bones, from those whose preface is necessary before we begin the remembrance of broken and diverse members of each one. And therefore I say that when you come across a fracture or dislocation or sprain or wound, then you must, at the beginning, apply yourself to phlebotomy or to leavening that, or whichever one, if no cause prohibits it, as is the weakness of virtue, or the one to whom you come across is a child, or old in decrepitude, or the time is of vehement heat, or of vehement cold.
And then be content in his food of cold herbs and of fowl meat and of crabs; and forbid thick meats and fill him with foods and all foods that fill the veins with blood, until you are sure of a cauterized abscess, and if you do not fear effusion of matter to the limb.
And then return the patient to his first regimen to which he is accustomed.
And therefore, as the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient must be fed with nutritious foods of nourishment much and heavy in which there is viscosity, such as foods made of cooked wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and fish, and cow bellies, and eggs, and fresh birds, and thick meat and similar to those, and with this regimen is made coagulation of fracture more lightly and better.
And know that the bones that are broken in men who are in the first youth and in the decrepit, it is not possible that they may be continued and consolidated according to their nature by the drying of their limbs.
But however, nature adjusts and amnesties upon the broken bone from every part any cause seeming to glut in which there is thickness that adheres and strengthens that until it is simultaneously conglutinated and tied together, until the end of fortitude and firmness comes, as fire at the beginning, until no cause prohibits it from its works.
And for this reason it is necessary that you pause the foods of the patient, foods in which there is heaviness and viscosity and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the species of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for the fracture of the bone of the leg is diverse from the fracture of the bone of the head; and the fracture of the bone of the foot is diverse from the fracture of the bone of the hand; and similarly all the other members are diverse among themselves.

Many signs of fracture of the bone. And by this is known a bone fracture: it is the twisting of it, and from that prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and so of it when you compress it with your hand.
And therefore when there is no apparent twisting in the place, nor is it felt when you touch the bone with agitation and the patient does not feel great pain, then it is not here a fracture.
However, it is possible that it is a small twist or fissure, and then you must feel it with your hand and with compression of the whole; however, place over it of the medicines of which remembrance is brought about at that moment the one which convenes to the place; and then be strait by subtle constriction.

Chapter Second: Of fracture when it happens in the head.
The species of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for of fractures some is done by percussion of a blow, and the percussion stops all the bone within the skull, which is left the bone, as if it were done in the shaft, and for this reason this species is named ascial; or the blow incides any part of the bone, and incides the flesh of it and does not pass the incision within the back of it; and this species of fracture is named levatio absolute.
And the plague of these two fractures or is great or little, and of fracture that is contricio or attrition; and cause of it is percussion with a club or the falling upon a stone, and their semblance.
And this fracture or is penetrating that already approaches the pannicol which is left the bone; or it is in the face of the bone, and the plague of these two fractures or is great or little.
And of fracture is that which is ammended in the bone, in the subtlety of the hair small, and for that reason this species is named fracture capillar.
And is fracture that is done or by falling or by percussion of a stone and its semblance, that makes enter some lamina of the bone to the parts within, and it is done in the place concavity as it happens to the payroles of ram when it happens to their percussion and it is part of their entrance to the parts within.
And mostly this is done in the bodies of wet bones, as if it were the heads of infants.
And to all these species fractures satagias, so are pains as if stars separated. And I will come with recollection of all that in its place.
And we shall know all these species of fracture by detection upon those and by inquiry of them with tentacles and ostament of corrupted flesh that is upon them.
And the species capillar is known by that which is discovered upon the bone, and it is abstergit, and lined upon it with tentacle, for the fracture appears black.
In the cure of a fracture, consider at the beginning the accidents of the patient, and if you see by the accidents that signify by manifest signification on the body, such as vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of understanding, and abscess of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and swelling of the joints and redness of those, and resemblances to those accidents in the major dispositions, then adopt no cure.
And if you see accidents that are not current, you hope for health, then you begin the cure of it.
The cause is that if it has come to you the afflicted at the beginning as it is afflicted and this is in the days of winter, then you must study in the state of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then you must have the bone studied before the seventh day, because that which is left of the bone be corrupted by the panniculus, and that the accidents that we have recounted occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the panniculus covering the brain, and is in small parts and attrition, then you must insert the minced part and rub it, according to that I will tell you.
And it is that you shave the head of the afflicted patient, and uncover over the bone according to custom and manner as you are able according to the figure of the plague, and that it be light upon the patient.
And if it happens to you to detect the bone, flows of blood or abscess caut, then you oppose it with what you have, and it is that you fill the place with cloths infused in wine and olive oil rubbed, and make the plague rest until the abscess is seated, and you are sure of the blood flow; and then you begin in the circuition of the bone and in the ablation of it.
And this is according to one of two ways of operation.

And use them with ease in percussion on the inscissori so that it does not break the head and damage it.

And they are not called thus but because those do not pause the term of the bone of the test to that which is near it, because that the drill has a round extremity or margin over that which is left the head of it sharp seeming to margin or to small circle, the which prohibits being submerged and passing the thickness of the bone.

And this is the form of three species of drills: The quality of perforating in the circuit of the broken bone is that you pause the drill on the bone and revolve that with your fingers until you know that the bone is already penetrated; and then change the drill to another place, and pause that which is between all trauc the quantity of the thickness of the ray or around that.

And if the peritoneum is cut when you eradicate the bone, then when you take cloth according to the quantity of the wound, and submerge that in rose oil, and place that over the orifice of the wound; and then take another cloth folded or tripled and submerge it in wine and rose oil and place that over the first cloth and do this as lightly as you can so that you do not injure the scalp; and then administer over that bandage lightly, and do not tighten it except for the quantity that the wound has alone.
And leave that with this care for a day or two until you are sure of the abscess caut; and then untie that and then administer medicines with which is superfluous dryness, such as roots of flax and flour of herbs, and powder of incense and of aristolochia, and similar to those.
And it is that you make of these medicines powder that be pulverized over the wound, as they are dry; and universally, administer in that all medicine of property of which is that it abstergesca and does not bite.
And guard yourself according to your virtue that the wound in your cure be clean; and not be in it dirtiness of rinsing or other; nor let virulence be added to it; for when it is adjusted over the peritoneum of the brain, it corrupts that and you can destroy that, and happens to the patient a evil of malignant evil.
And happens in the scalp of the head, as from it is modified the bone and most when the care of it is lessened, and it is blackness on the surface of it; and therefore consider then if it happens to the patient the accidents that we have said, know that it is pernicious all of it.
And if it does not happen blackness but from the medicine that is placed under it, and it is in virtue of that medicine that makes that blackness, then when you take of honey one part and of rose oil parts three, and in those lines a cloth, and then place that over the scalp; and then cure that with the species of cure that convenes until it is healed.
Chapter III.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken but two parts together, or one of those sometimes, for in it you have two bones, for the part left is cartilaginous, and it is not broken and does not happen to it but attrition and tortuosity and sinuosity.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then when you introduce the said little one into its nose, and that you align that fracture inside with your said index, and with the said point outside, until the nose returns according to its natural figure. And be done that by you with ease, and guard yourself that no pain be caused to the patient by that your operation.
And if the fracture is in the most sovereign part of the nose, and the said one does not reach to that, then when it is equal to the extremity of the ray in which it is small thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the semblance of that.
And take care of it in the restoration of it on the first day of the fracture, if it is possible; and if not, then about the seventh or tenth of the rest of the abscess caut.
And then introduce into the extremity of the nose a piece of cloth of it, if the fracture is in one part, or put two splints, if the fracture is in two parts, and be in the splint thickness of as much quantity that it fills the channel of the nose.
And I say some of the ancient restorers that the splints be enclosed in border, and be changed every day, but it is not seen to me; have that the piece be enclosed in album of comfit with powder of flour; and then leave the qualgua until the bone is set and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes it is in the nose, in place of a splint, a pain of a boil that is surrounded by a lion's cloth; and if you already have the fracture of the nose very violent and that the patient is not prohibited from breathing.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you want, do this, and if you want, do splints.
And if another time a boil occurs, apply a plaster to the nose with cerot or with a cloth soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or with diaquilon.
But if no boil occurs, then cover that with a plaster of sesame flour and powdered incense mixed with egg white; and then place a lion's cloth over it, and do not touch the nose with any reason.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted, then cover those, and extract them with instruments that are suitable for those; and then close the fissure, and cure it with what hardens and consolidates it, and with plasters suitable for it.
And if a plague occurs in the nose, then cover it with splints and administer lead pipes until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
Of the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the jaw is broken and the fracture of it is not with a plague, then regard if the fracture is only outwardly, and not broken in two and curved at the parts inside, then its knowledge is lighter.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, then you introduce your left index into the mouth of the patient; and similarly if the fracture is on the left mandible, then introduce the right index finger; and push with that the inward flexibility of the fracture with ease to the outer parts, with your other hand from the outside of the bone; and with that gently equalize it.
And if the jaw fracture is already separated into two, then you must administer extension of two parts according to rectitude until it is possible the equation of it.
And if there occurs concussion of teeth to disruption, then strengthen those of which you hope will remain with a thread of gold or silver or lead; and then place a cerot over the broken jaw, and then place a doubled cloth over it, and place a large thick splint over the cloth; or a piece of sole equal to the length of the jaw; and then tie it together according to how the ligation of it is prepared and how the application is suitable, until it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and put his food in lion's broth.
And if you estimate that the figure has already been altered in any way, then you untie it on the third day; and then rectify what has already been altered from it and plaster it with powdered moly, with egg white, or with sesame flour, because you will have removed the cerot from it, and place wool over the plaster.
And thus, as it stays annexed and adjusted that plaster over it, and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave it, that you do not untie it until it is healed and strengthens the fracture; because many times this fracture is straightened in three weeks.
And if another time a boil occurs, then administer what we have said many times in the sedation of it until it is healed, and may that boil be removed.
And if the fracture is with a plague, then consider if it is a piece of bone separated or splinters, then you subtilize that you remove those splinters with what is suitable for it in the removal of them from the instruments.
And if the orifice of the plague is strait, then you enlarge it with the fleubotomist according to the quantity of your need.
And after you have removed those splinters, and of those there is no boil remaining, then you close the orifice of the plague if it is wide; and if not, place over it one of the plasters that are suitable for it and harden it, until it is healed.
Chapter V.
Of the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Many times the clavicle is broken at the front part of the muscle, and that fracture is made in one of three ways.
Because it is broken, or separated, or is separated into two parts one of which does not occur splinters; and it is easy to restore it; or it is so that it occurs splinters in it, and it is difficult to restore it; or it is so that the fracture is with a plague.
And the operation in that, when the fracture is with a plague, is that you have two ministers present, so that one holds the adjutory and follows the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck with that part.
And then engulf the fracture with your fingers until the form of it is made according to what is necessary, and that there is little eminence or concavity in it.
And if you need more extension, then place beneath the patient's armpit a support of cloth or wool, and let its size be according to the quantity of your need; and extend and elevate the clavicle and hold the support with your hand until you engulf the fracture according to what is necessary.
And if you cannot pull the extremity of the clavicle outwards, because it has reached the shoulder, then have the patient lie on their back, and place a cushion of medium size on the muscle of their neck.
And the minister presses the muscle of their neck towards the lower parts while the bone of the clavicle is elevated that is in the shoulder, and then you straighten the fracture and enclose it with your hands.
But if you feel splinters of the clavicle and they are bad, then have the patient lie on their back and extract those splinters with ease.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you make an incision of it with one of the scissors which you have prepared for this purpose, because you have placed below the clavicle the instrument that serves the forceps; and it is an instrument of wood or iron of which this is the shape:
And it is similar to a spoon of which this is the concavity; and its width according to what you need it for, according to the size of the bone or its smallness.
But the length of it be according to what is possible to you in the operation; that it have two extremities thus as sometimes, and one extremity be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the plaque which you have incised when you have extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and it is certain of abscess caut, then you adjust the two lips of the plaque with suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear abscess caut, then you fill the plaque with cloths and powders according to the quantity of the scissura of the plaque.
And if an abscess caut occurs, then you place a cloth in vinegar and rose oil and wine, and place it over it.
And all, that the splint not be bad over the broken bone; and for so that you see the patient every day, that when the ligament is softened or you see the splint already remaugud, then you rectify that and strengthen the ligation.
And then place the sleep of the patient on their neck, and place below the tip of it of their sleep at night a small cushion; so that with that it be elevated the assistant of it from the side of it, and therefore is elevated the fracture of the clavicle to elevation of the muscle; or be the arm of it tied to the neck of it.
And do not untie the ligation if you do not find in the place proximate or abscess, within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you have seen the face of it to that, and adjust in the stricture and restore that and leave it until it be restored and be coagulated the fracture of the clavicle.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the measure in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter VI.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the shoulder.
The spatula tart is broken in the side of it and not broken of it but the margins.
And therefore when it is broken the place of it — and it is broken in the middle place of it — and it is not known except by touch, then according to what is the figure of the fracture forces you the preparation of it and reduce that according to the natural figure in every manner of engine which is to you possible.
And then place on the place powder of wheat with egg white and linen cloth; and then place powder of cloth doubled; and then place over it a sheet of subtle wood according to the quantity of the spatula all or perhaps a little.
But if below the sheet is concavity in some of the places of the spatula, then you enclose that concavity with linen cloth, and then raise the sheet according to equality.
And then strengthen above with a long humidity with decent constriction and with firmness of the sheet until it not be remaugud from its place.
And consider the ligation every day; and every time that it is softened, tie another time and strengthen it and equal the sheet if it is remaugud from its place.
And place the patient on their side.
And the spatula is restored in twenty days or in twenty-five.
And therefore when this number is complete, then you dissolve the ligation, and that you know for sure that that is of the bones of which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the place of the broken bone and place the bandage below the hip, then you cut above it, and remove it, and do as we have said in the fracture of the clavicle of a suppurating abscess, if it happens here.
Chapter VII.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot sometimes is broken in the middle part of it and that is quite bad; but the extremities of it must be moved that they are cicatrized and comminuated.
And of the accidents of it when it is broken in the middle, it is that it declines to the parts of it and it happens to it great pain, and difficulty in the hale, and cough, and great heat and vomiting of blood; and it is declared in it cavity in the broken bone and it is not hidden to the feeling.
And the restoration of it is that it be laid the patient on his back, and place between his shoulder blades a cushion; and then press on the muscles of it, and adjust the bones of it with the hands of two sides, and subtly in the operation of it and in the equation according to every manner that is to this possible and that convenes to this, until that returns the figure of the bone according to that cove.
And then place on it a plaster and cotton and place above the table of a subtle cloth of gauze or of calico and of their likeness in levitat, because you envelop that in cloth; and then subtly in lian that on the broken bone that it not be harmful; and go to the ligation according to roundness to the back sometimes, and strengthen that with a decent stricture; and then consider the ligation in every hour, and when it is softened strengthen that.
And if necessity provokes the disgorgement of that you must corrode or puss, or pain, or abscess that happens in the place, then you cut and untie that and eradicate the plaster and rectify that which happens by that in a manner of cure of it; and then reduce the plaster, if times to that the face; and adjust to it stricture until that it be healed.
Chapter VIII.
Of the restoration of the back when it is broken.
Know that fracture of the back does not catch but in places gross that follow the back; and of the extremities of them of the part before it does not happen to those contricio but by that which they are cartilaginous. And cognition of it is not hidden to the feeling, it is the inquiry of it with the said.
And the restoration is thus that you equal the fracture with the said according to the manner possible, until that it be engulfed according to that cove, and then plaster and strengthen the broken bone with steel, if it be needed of that.
And if the fracture of the back is declining to the parts of it, then it happens to the patient great pain and puncture, as well as puncture that happens to him who has pleureci, and by that which the bones put the veil; and it happens to it difficulty of handling it, and cough, and vomiting of blood; and this is of difficult cure.
And already the Ancients were ingenious in that with many ingenious things.
And of theirs it is that they say: whoever places food in the patient that engendered inflation and flatulence, because the belly be inflated and be extended, and the fracture be impelld to the parts outside.
But we would not advise that for that it not be cause lighter to generate abscess caut, if it does not happen; and if it already happens, then it already helps to that.
And they said some of them that it be placed upon a ventouse and then that it be sucked with force; and it is seeming to reasoning, but however it is feared that the ventouse drives the superfluities to the place by the weakness.
And they said some of them: whoever the place be covered with wool that already is submerged in hot oil, and be placed powders between the back until that it be filled, that it be the ligation equal when it envelops that according to roundness; and then be cured the patient with cure of pleurisy of food and of medicine.
And if a vehement cause constricts the patient which does not suffer, and the bones put the veil with puncture leading, they fear upon the patient, then whoever they cut above the place, uncover of the broken rib, and then place above that instrument that conserve the ciphasc, the remembrance of which is preceded, and that they cut the bones with facility, and extract that.
And then that they adjust the two lips of the wound, if it is great, with sewing, and cure that with plasters until that it be healed.
And if it happens of it abscess, then you cut and put powders in hot oil and place upon the place, and that it be cured the patient with that which cove to the abscess of it.
And be laid on the side upon which he sleeps, until that it be healed.
Chapter IX.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the vertebra of the back and of the neck.
Sometimes it happens fracture to the bones of the neck, and it is quite bad to them, and seeming to the vertebrae of the back. And as that happens to some and you want to know if it heals or no, then consider, because if sometimes its hands already softened and stupefied and dead and not can more those nor extend nor strengthen those and when you constrict those with your said or prick those with needle and not feel in those pain, then you know that it is not healed, and in the worst dispositions it is mortal.
And if the patient feels constriction with the fingers and puncture, then you know that the neck of the bone is saved and that the patient recovers with treatment.
And if you see in the vertebrae of the neck the appearance of this and want to know if it is healing or not, then consider the feet of the patient.
Then if you see that they are already softened, apply to those those things that are applied to the hands, and as soon as you place them on their back, there will be ventilation and expulsion if it is their will, and sometimes it is reversed on their back and wants to urinate and cannot, then you know that it is mortal, and therefore do not work in the cure of him.
But if you do not see any cause of those things, the cause is better and less severe.
And the cure of him is that you pierce to the seat of the abscess cautery that you place on the affected vertebra rubbed with oil, or with poultices of eggs; place on that some days until the abscess is cauterized.
And then place one of the comforting and existing plasters, and strengthen it with a bandage; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility and that he does not sleep on the part with which he feels pain until it is healed.
And if you see the separation of the bone from the flesh or any cause that is already separated from it, then cover that with a bandage and keep that bone; and then adjust the two lips of the wound, with suture if it is large; and then care for it with some kind of ointment of the intestines, until it is healed.
But if the tailbone of the coccyx is broken, and it is the end of the pelvis, then cover that with a bandage and keep that bone; and then adjust the two lips of the wound, with suture if it is large; and then care for it with some kind of ointment of the intestines, until it is healed.
And if you feel broken splints in it, then cover it, and keep it, and cure the wound with the cure that was preceded until it is healed.
Chapter X.
Of the restoration of the anus.
The bones of the anus are broken, and if they break, then the fracture of them is nothing but a communication in the extremities of them and they are cut in the length and incline to the parts of the sides; and if you see the patient pain in the place and puncture, and the leg is stunned when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture as is the figure of it.
And if the fracture is in the extremity of it so only, then you align that according to what appears to you by equality align until it is assembled to its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in the long or that already inclines to the parts below, then you make the patient lie on their belly until it is prepared for you the restoration of that fracture. And therefore when you have aligned it, place on it plaster, and then place on it a bandage of splint or of bandage, and strengthen by stricture on which you do not fear puncture of fracture nor removal of the splint.
And align the concavity with what fills that until that presses the stricture according to equality, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their side.
But if you see abscess caut, then you cease from extension of it and its restoration until the abscess is cauterized, and place on it what seats it according to what is preceded; and then return to the restoration of it, and strengthen it thus as you cover.
And if you see in the bone splints or are comminuted, then you treat them.
Chapter XI.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the adductor.
Adductor is that which is between the hip into the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then the restoration of it is in one of two ways, of which is one that you take a splint of medium thickness according to this form:
And tie in two extremities two bandages, and then let it be suspended from the elevated place.
And follow the patient on a chair; and then throw out his broken arm over the splint until it reaches the tip of it in the middle place of which is annexed the curvature of the splint.
And then suspend on it some heavy cause, or the minister extends it to the parts below; and then the physician and that the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns as it covers.
And the other way is that the patient be reversed on his neck and suspend the arm of the neck with a bandage; and then command two ministers that one of them holds what is above the fracture with his hands; and the other holds the pus below of it; and each of them extends to its part.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you strengthen over the fracture with a bandage and over that with another bandage, and each of the two ministers extend to their two parts.
And therefore if the fracture is close to the extremity of the muscle, then cover that the half of the bandage be placed over the hip and the other below the fracture of the hip.
And similarly if the fracture is near the hip, then it should be placed the ligament over that place and over that hip.
Hold that firmly and work it, and as soon as it is well wrapped according to what is covered and applied for good application, then strengthen it so that it does not accidentally happen abscess.
But if an abscess occurs, then loosen the constriction on the seventh day, and place on it wool soaked in vinegar and olive oil until the abscess heals, and then tighten it again.
And the way to tighten it is that you place a plaster on the fracture, and then place a layer of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm on that support, and place your hand open on their muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten it over the support of the arm so that the arm is in place of stars, if nothing prevents this from the broken bone.
And then use stars.
And it is that you place over the fracture that star that is pus wider and pus stronger of the other stars; and place between each star the width of the finger, and let the length of the stars be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers of all part.
And then tighten over the stars with the constriction which I have said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your constriction over the place of the fracture be pus tight. And as much as the fracture is longer, the constriction be smaller.
And therefore if you see the situation of the stars and the constriction as we have said in the hour in which you restore the limb to its situation, then you do.
And if you fear abscess, then you loosen the constriction and the stars on the seventh day as we have said.
And then consider the ligament in all parts for three days so that nothing happens in the place proximately or inflammation; or is prohibited the food to arrive at the limb by disposition of superfluity of constriction; and therefore rectify all that as we have said.
But if you are on confidence that nothing will happen from this, then do not untie the ligature until after three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on their back, and their hands on their stomach.
And place on the support a cushion full of wool well wrapped, and consider in every hour of the night or of the day that the figure of the broken limb be not loosened or dissolved, or that the ligament be softened, therefore rectify that all with your virtue.
And place the patient's food according to the order which we have mentioned before, so that the food be subtle at the beginning while it is begun to tighten; and then cover that the food be thickened of it.
Because of custom of the support and the bed of it is that they be strengthened for forty days; and then cover that they be untied and be administered bath and be medicated with ointments which rectify that.
And if the fracture is laid smooth, then you do not untie from that the ligament and the stars within a days or within two months.
Chapter .xii.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two focils; of which one is small, and that is the one that lies the finger well, and the other is large and that is the one that is placed below the little finger, from the part further down.
And strong that is broken the major focil only, or the small, and strong that are broken together.
And when the small focil above is broken, then the restoration of it is lighter, and the healing of it is lighter.
And when the focil of it below is broken the fracture of it is bad, and the healing of it is difficult.
And worse of that is when the two bones are broken together.
Therefore if the bone which is broken is the small focil above, then it is convenient to the physician, he must the restoration of it, that place small extension with facility until that be well wrapped.
But if the large focil is that which is broken, then cover that place extension more vehement.
And if the two focils are together broken, then cover that you place the constriction more strong.
And cover that you place the figure of the hand the restoration of it and the extension of it extended over a cushion, and let the wrist of the hand be at the parts above, more high than all the other fingers, and the little finger be lower than the other fingers, and the patient be centered on a pillow laid down.
And let there be a cushion before it in the height or upright of it, so that the patient does not lean work.
And then the minister extends the arm from the part further down with his hand or ligament, and another minister extends above similarly.
And then the physician wraps the bones while reducing that according to the best figure that he can.
And if in the fracture of the bone there are splinters, then you pierce to reduce all that in its place with your virtue.
And if there appears in him a separated hernia that pierces the groin and is not in your hope of its restoration, then you must place it and remove it according to the manner which we have said before.
But if the fracture is with a plaque, I have already made a singular chapter about this, and therefore take the cure of that.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then you line a cloth with cerate made of rose oil and white wax, and it should be of medium thickness between fineness and roughness, and strengthen the cloth above it with a subtle structure until the abscess is subsided; and then remove the cerate and place an emplastre prepared of powder of ground clay mixed with egg white, and then place stars; and let the star be the one placed over the fracture, pus stronger and pus wider.
And know that the number of the stars of the arm is six in the major dispositions, or whether the fracture is in one focus or in two foci together.
And place your structure over the place of the fracture, more strongly and more vehemently, and when the structure is more situated to the parts above the fracture or to the parts lower than it, place the structure a little softer according to that which is preceded the remembrance of it in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that are enveloped over the fracture should be soft and humid cloths, and not hard or rough, and the thread with which it is strengthened should be of the proper thickness between fine and coarse, as we have recounted.
And consider the limb and the joint after a few days. And if any cause occurs of which rectification is necessary, as well as prudently the one that occurs to the limb, and then cover that you enwrap the limb with warm water until that prudency ceases.
And let the limb not be constricted until it rests, and then return the structure.
And if the structure is already softened and the bone is already remade and seems like those, then rectify all that with your virtue.
And consider more if the nourishment is prohibited to reach the limb by superfluity of structure, for then cover that you soften it a little and let it for a few days until it runs to that the nourishment, and then strengthen it.
But if the patient does not occur any of those causes that we have said, then cover that it should not be detached within about twenty days or thereabouts.
And then let the hand of the patient be suspended by its virtue of movement agitation, and make that sleep on its back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days or in thirty-two days, or very soon it is restored in twenty-eight days, and all that is according to the disposition of the complexities and the dispositions of the virtue.
Chapter .xiii.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the thumb and the fingers.
Know that to the thumb and to the knuckles of the fingers, tart it often happens a fracture, and it does not occur to them but attrition many times.
And when a fracture or attrition occurs to the hand, then cover that the patient should follow lying flat and in front of him lies the cloth according to equality, and then place the hand of him over that extended.
And then the minister extends the broken bones and the physician aligns those until they are conjoined with good conjoining.
And then cover that you place plaster and stopa, if no abscess occurs; and then place stars above according to the quantity of the place, and you have already enveloped that in a cloth of it.
But if the fracture is in the parts of the fingers of the palm, and then make a semblance of a sphere of cloth, and command the patient to strengthen that with his fractured palm; then let it be strengthened with a long cloth.
And let the star be of leather with cloth in which it is levitated, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and strengthen that according to what cover.
But if the fracture is in the outer parts, then cover that you place stars above and another star of the lower part in the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then place the structure, as if the hand is rolled to the wrist between the fingers and joints.
And if a fracture occurs to any of the knuckles of the fingers, then if the finger is placed, it should be aligned according to what cover and then strengthened with the palm and if you want, place a small straight star to it so that the fracture is elevated and not marred.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, as in that of the middle location, or in the index, or in the auricular, or in the annular, then it should be aligned and tied with the finger that is next to it; or all of them should be tied according to continuity, and it is better; or place on it a small star, as we have said in the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and then that, the disposition of abscesses and oppose — with your operation — to that which cover when any cause of those occurs, according to that which is iterated the narration of it.
Chapter .xiv.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the bone of the hip.
The bone of the hip many times is broken and is declared to the sentiment according to that it is converted to the part in front and to the most sovereign.
And the restoration of him is done in such a way that the ligament is tightened over the fracture and another ligament is left below the fracture, and the patient is prone on his belly and face.
And then the minister extends all the ligament to its part according to the quality, when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is located at the root of the thigh, then the ligament is pulled towards the knee at the point of the pelvis, so that the extension is on it, and the other ligament is below the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is located at the knee, then the ligament is pulled towards the knee, and the extension is to the parts below.
And then the doctor aligns the bones with both his hands until it is reduced according to the natural likeness and the bone is joined by a good connection; then cover with plaster and a splint, if not, abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs in it, then you leave it for a few days until the abscess has subsided, and then return to your cure.
The splint of it is made like this: cover over the fracture a hard bandage, and fold it two or three times, and remove the superfluity from it; and then wrap the leg until it reaches the thigh and the root of the thigh.
And introduce a long cord between the thigh and the leg near the knee to the parts below, and the ends of the cord come from the sovereign part of both parts, and then roll over the leg and thigh what superfluity of the bandage.
And then place on the thigh in the place of that fracture splints, and place one splint on the bone of the leg.
And then fill the vacancies between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the splint is coagulated.
And then begin with a strong splint on the middle of the fracture with three or four wrappings, and as much as you move away with the ligament from the place of the fracture, your splint should be smaller and lighter and softer.
And then extend both ends of the cord to the parts below until it reaches the ankle of the foot, and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side so that the ligament is not removed from its place.
And then leave the splint on it, so that it does not occur to the limb corruption or abscess or inflammation and similar to those; and if any cause of those occurs, then cut it from it, and rectify what occurs to it all again, according to what we have said many times.
But if in the bone is the weight of the bone which is placed, then cover that according to what is possible to you; and if not, then cut over it and remove that, and cure with that of which the remembrance precedes until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is tightened with another thing which are adjusted to it splints, as we have said in the adjutant and in the arm.
But however in the restoration of it in such a manner made do not occur to the patient lameness; and if it is restored alone, then another thing which is applied to the other leg, then it is necessary that he limp from the one which is always.
And know that the thigh is tightened in 40 days or a few more or a few less, and all that is according to the diversity of the complexities and of the other dispositions.

Chapter XVI.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the leg.
Know that in the leg there are two bones of which one is thick, and it is called by the name of the tibia; and the other is thin, and it is called fibula.

And if the two bones are broken together, the leg is converted to all parts.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is converted to the parts in front.
And if the largest bone is broken, then support it, and those are the reasons why it is not your friend.
Then administer extension, flexion, and ligation of the splints according to that alignment.
However, if the fracture of the leg is light and has many splints, then the extension should be less and lighter, and alleviate with your virtue.

Chapter XXIII.
On the subject of dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any of the joints of the limb, so that it is prohibited from movement, and the figure of the limb is distorted, and it causes great pain and injuries to the patient.
Therefore, when an accident occurs to any dislocation, it is necessary to immediately reduce it, and not delay that reduction, because if it is delayed, the limb is suppurated and very difficult to reduce the dislocation.
And therefore, it should not be bad or stretched during the time of suppuration, because often it brings spasms and intense pain.
But when this happens, then it is necessary to apply a poultice to the suppurating limb, and let it rest until the suppuration ceases; and then the limb should be bandaged with warm water and oil, and then it should be reduced with facility.
And every limb should be cured with that which the remembrance will come back to its place.
And you have already ordered the chapters of dislocation according to what precedes in fracture; from the part of the body most sovereign to the part most inferior.
Chapter XXIV.
On the cure of dislocation of the lower jaw.
So the two jaws are dislocated if not tart; and the dislocation of both is made in one of two ways: that they are pulled out of their places by a small movement, and are softened a little; or they are dislocated by a complete and complicated dislocation, so that they are softened at the junction until the saliva of the patient flows and that he cannot hold it or close his mouth, and his tongue is enveloped in his word.
But when the dislocation of it is slight, then in the major disposicions it returns by itself with little cause.
But if the dislocation is complete and complicated, then it is necessary to administer its reduction gently, and not to delay it at all.
And that the minister has the head of the patient, and the doctor inserts the tip of his finger into the root of the dislocation of the lower jaw from inside his mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he inserts both of his fingers at once, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of him are outside with which he pulls; and he commands the patient to cease his jaws and allow them to go from any part; and the doctor pulls the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated at the same time, then administer vaporization with warm water and oil, until the reduction of both is made lighter and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And therefore, when they return and are pulled together, and the mouth of the patient is closed and not softened, then place on them powders of cloth with cerot, and make it of wax and oil rubbed; and then apply it gently with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and his head be standing between two pillows, so that he does not strain either to the right or to the left; and do not force him to chew for any cause; having his food be forbidden to him, until when the pains are reduced and the jaws are knitted, then he may chew what he wants.
And that be administered with facility, and do not work on the opening of the mouth, food and drink and conversation, until the jaw is knitted and healed.
And if the reduction of two jaws when they are dislocated at any hour and are not converted to their places, then many times this happens fever and persistent cough, and very much the belly of the patient is alarmed, and very much and vomits pure bile.
And when you see this, then you know that it is pernicious; and many times that one dies who has this in 10 days.

Chapter XXVI.

Chapter XXIX.

Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in every part.
And when a finger is dislocated of those to the back of the palm or to the belly of it, then stretch the finger and push the dislocation of it with your thumbs, until it returns.
And then support the head of the finger, and suspend that from the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
And then suspend that and extend that until straight it is healed in that day.
And when it will be night, tie that according to that narration, and do not cease to untie that in the day and exercise with movement, and tie that at night; and do this for some days until it is straight.
And do similar with this, if it is dislocated to the belly of the arm, and tie that to that part and do with this your first operation, until it is healed.
And similarly do with this when it is dislocated to the other parts.
Chapter XXX.
Of the cure of dislocations of the vertebrae of the back.
When any of the vertebrae of the back or of the neck happens dislocation complicated, and so remaugutz motz vertebrae from their places, then in that one is no cure, because death takes hold of the patient.
And a sign of this is that the digestion of the patient is outside of his will, and cannot retain that; and many times are softened some members of it or two legs of it or two arms of it, or one of those; or is remaugut a vertebra from its place, because many times is remaugut and many times is made the removal of those to four parts.
Therefore, that one is already remaugut to the parts behind and is called gelbozitat.
But the cure of it is that then consider if the gelbozitat happens from puerility, because then in that one is no cure nor of that one is no healing of all.
But in that one that comes from a blow or from percussion or similar to those, already said the Ancients the manners of cure with sermons prolixs, of which the most returns with prophecy; but I already and shortened of that of which the little excuse of the too much of it, for the my better proximity to the better understanding exposition, with which I have here exposed and formed ex instrument divers of that with which I will show this and explain that.
And of those no is cured unless that one that happens in the back properly with that which I have recomposed.
And it is that the patient be extended on his face over a pug even prop of wall, and be extended below him instrument molh so that not be lifted the feet of him.
And then a straight stick be placed, fixed in the ground in the extremity of the pug around the head of it, and another stick in the air the feet of it in the other extremity of the pug.
And the minister has the stick, and not be held in fossa; and another minister has the other according to that form.
And then wrap a bandage around the patient's head and leave the linen bandage firm; and extend the extremity of the bandage to the shaft which is the head of it, and tie it there; and then tighten the other bandage around the patient's hips, and tie it to the legs; and then adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other shaft which is the feet.
And then the minister, having wrapped the bandage to the part of the shaft, and not being removed the two shafts and the bandages of their places in which they are placed, but however not being fastened as we have said.
And the doctor places his palms on the spine with strength while he returns, or who places upon that a table, and then that adjusts upon the table his feet while he returns.
But if it does not return in this way, then take a table of which the latitude or longitude is in the air of three arms, and carve in the wall of which we have said that is near the patient a place in which enters the extremity of the table. And then place the majority of the table upon the spine, and the doctor places his feet upon the other extremity, and tightens well while the spine is shortened and that it returns to its place.
And if you want, open with a turn which is revolved with the hand; and it is that you place on the ground behind the pug two shafts, the length of which is of an arm, and behind the head of it two shafts; and let the length that is between each shaft be a palm, and already it is made in each shaft a trough in which runs the turn, and each one be fastened to the ground so that it is not bad.
And let there be also a round shaft in which is the turn, and be revolved in the two troughs of the two shafts.
And in the extremity of it is a trough in which is formed the shaft, the length of which is a palm with that which is revolved; and in the two other shafts is similar to that.
And then tighten the bandages that you have tightened in the feet of the patient in the trough which is the head of it, and the bandages that you have tightened in the thighs of it in the turn which is the feet of it.
And then place each turn and stay a minister who twists the turn with his hand, and the doctor aligns the spine according to what we have said.
And as soon as the spine is returned, and the place is aligned, then cover with a paste made with dried album of egg, and then cotton; and then place on the paste a table of which the latitude is three digits or about those, and the length of it is the quantity that reaches the place of the yellowness, and on the part of the spine it is.
And then tie it with a bandage as we have said, and use the patient of subtle nourishment while he is healing.
And if any imminence remains in the place at the end of the healing, then cover that it be administered medicines that soften and cleanse with administration of the table for a long time, which we have recommended; and already it is administered in that place a sheet of lead.
And some times there happens imminence in the rear of the spines of the two, and it is cured by that dislocation; and it is that bone which already threatens because it should not be done proscription to it with this medicine, because very strong it will make you happen death.
Chapter .XXXI.
Of the cure of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle should not be a dislocation only, and it does not happen to those that happen to the other joints of small movement and concavity.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated according to four manners, because it is dislocated to the front parts, or to the outside parts, or to the front parts, or to the back parts.
And mostly it is dislocated to the inside parts, and few to the front parts or to the back parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it to the inside parts is that, when you adjust the leg of the healthy patient with the patient, it is longer and the thigh is more prominent than of the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend his foot in the direction of the knee, and it is the place that sees the knee joint inflamed with a cautery abscess, so that the head of the thigh has already arrived here.
And the sign of that one which happens dislocation is to the outside parts, is that the accidents of it are contrary to this accident.
And the sign of that one which happens dislocation to the front parts is that it expands its thigh according to compliance, but however it does not bend that which is pain in the thigh, and if it is forced to go and cannot go over that to the front parts; and the urine of it is retained and the groin of it is inflamed, and because of the ambulation of it upon the heel.
And the sign of that one which happens dislocation to the back parts is that it does not expand the thigh, nor can it bend that which bends the groin, and the thigh of it is shorter than the other, and the groin is softened, and the head of the thigh is in that place.
But the reduction of the types of this dislocation is that you consider, therefore, if the dislocation is old and antique, which is already fixed with the having that near you administer reduction of it and remains according to its disposition, then it is not in that cure of all; and therefore no hope that in that be made presumption.
But in that of which the dislocation is recent, and is one of the four species of dislocation, then take care that you turn the joint and extend it to the parts within and to the parts outside, and move it to the right and left, for it is very likely to return and does not need other cure.
But if it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare a strong minister and extend the leg of him from the farthest part, or with his hands, or with a bandage which is tied above the knee; and another minister extends the hand of him from below so that he puts his hands below the thighs of him; and then tighten with a bandage on the root of the thigh, and hold another minister the extremity of the bandage.
And that extension of him or from the part within to the angle of the shoulder, or from the part behind to the part of the back.
And that extension of all those in one go while the patient is elevated with his body from the ground and that remains hanging.
And this species of extension is special common to all four.
And therefore if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then in every species it is necessary to do what I will say of the proper cure.
In the reduction of it proper, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is at the parts within, then the patient must be laid on his side; and then place the bandages on the root of the thigh in that place which is between the head of the thigh and the place that is below the shoulder; and then extend the bandage from the part of the shoulder to the highest part of the shoulder blade.
And then another minister strongly presses the arms of him, and hold the thick place of the thigh of the patient and extend to the parts outside by violent extension, for that returns to its place.
And this species is of lighter cure with which this limb is reduced than the others.
But if it is excused on you and you do not obey the entry with this species of cure of all, then you must tie both legs of the patient together with a strong, light bandage, on both the ankles and both knees.
And the length of each of its parts by the quantity of four fingers, and the leg of the patient extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
And then the patient be suspended over the head of the pole, and that be in the middle; and the length of it from the ground the quantity of two arms.
And then command a strong young man to pull the head of the thigh, and that be suspended with the patient another young man, and the other young man pulls the thigh with strength and impulsion, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
But in the proper reduction, when the dislocation is at the part outside, then the patient must lie on his stomach according to what we have recommended in the having gelbozitat; and be tightened the bandage on the thigh of the patient properly and on the feet of him.
And then two poles be placed, one at the feet of him, and the other at the head; and then be placed poles adjusted to the middle of the arm put on which wet cloths be enveloped, so that the patient be not damaged because the pole is between the thighs of him, for that the extension to the parts below be not pulled.
And then each minister extends to his part, and the doctor with his hand engulfs the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is at the parts in front, then the leg must be extended slightly, and it is according to this same narration about arm; and the doctor places the palm of his right hand on the shoulder of the patient, and then pulls that with the other hand, and with that pull places that extended to the parts below to the part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is at the parts behind, then the patient must not be extended to the parts below, and it is elevated above the ground; having must be placed on hard cause, as much as the  eyssament if the width of him is dislocated at the parts outside, according to what we have said of the jasement of him on two widths; and it is on his face and the bandages be extended, as we have said, there is no harm.
And that compression be administered with a board on the place where the joint is.
Therefore, as the reduction is complicated according to what you must—the sign of the reduction of him is not hidden from you, and it is that you extend from the patient two legs, and when you see those enguals and the patient extends his leg and expands that ses excusacio, then you know that the limb according to what you must is returned—and then you adjust two cushions and put plaster and extend with bandage so that the width is not damaged in any of the parts; and adhere the patient to rest for three days or for four.
And then untie the ligament and the bandage, and compare one leg to the other.
Now, if you see those bones in place, then you know the dislocation is already fixed, and therefore let the patient go.
And if you see any cause for softening, then you return and bandage that, and extend that according to your first structure, and let it be for three days.
And then untie that, and make that person delay in walking for a few days, until it is comfortable.
Chapter .xxxii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the finger.
The finger is dislocated in three ways, which is dislocated to the outside parts, and to the inside parts, and to the back parts more to the front parts; and to the front parts it is not dislocated.
And the sign of dislocation of it is that you command the patient to apply his leg to the thigh; then you know that the finger is dislocated.
And the restoration of all ways of it is that you make the patient straight, so that he already extends his leg, if he can above that; adhere your two to the face of it, and place his foot between your feet; and then make adhere the finger of it to your palms, and keep those between your fingers on the finger of it with strength, and another minister extends his leg while the finger returns to its place.
And the sign of the return of it is that his leg adheres to the thigh with light effortless.
And then bandage that and make the leg adhere to the thigh, and then tie those together with a bandage for three days or four, and then untie those.
And do not use the patient for walking for a few days, until it is healed and comfortable.
But if the reduction of it to you is excused, then administer strong extension with ligaments of which in the cure of the hernia preceding narrative.
Chapter .xxxiii.
Of the cure of the dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle some times is dislocated by a small movement, and some times is dislocated according to compliment, and the dislocation of it is made either to the inside parts or to the outside parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it is that you see the ankle inflated proceeding to the part to which it is dislocated.
But the cure of the dislocation of it is of light reduction, and it is that it be extended with ease with the hands and be aligned while it returns.
But in the cure of it when it is dislocated according to compliment, it is necessary that you make the patient straight, and have one minister strong and valiant of the back part of the two in the middle place of it.
And then you with your right hand the foot of the most sovereign part, and with your left hand of the most back part of the foot in the place of the heel.
And then you pull the foot to you with your right hand, and with the left two times, and then extend with the left; and you push the foot of the foot with the right towards the thigh, so that the leg works.
And do this twice according to what we have counted.
And then push the foot of the foot to the thigh the third time, and you are at the heel.
Therefore if it returns in one time or two according to this manner, and if you see the foot aligned, it is good; and if not, then over it iterate the operation, for that returns.
But if to you is forbidden the reduction of it with what we have recounted, then make the patient lie on his back on the ground, and stretch a piece of cloth on the ground firm, and that it lies between the thighs of it, and you already have enveloped over it cloth so that it does not hurt the patient.
And then the minister of it holds its thighs, and another minister extends the leg with its hands or with a ligament that lies on the collar of the foot; and then extend that each minister against extension of its part, and the cloth be extended between the thighs of the patient retaining it, so that the body of it not be pulled to the back parts towards its extension.
And then the doctor aligns the dislocation with its hands, and another minister holds the healthy thigh to the back parts, for the dislocation returns with ease.
And therefore when the dislocation returns and is declared to you the health of the reduction of it, then pause plaster and extend that with strong ligaments, and tie the foot with a ligament to the thigh.
And that you fear or take care of the nerves that are behind on the ankle, so that the ligament over it is not vehement and hurts it strongly.
And then leave that for two days or three; and if the ligament is softened, extend that.
And then untie that on the third day or the fourth, and forbid the patient from movement for forty days. But if it is forced to go before that space, it is not done securely that the dislocation not be over it dissolved and corrupted, and then that which does not receive cure.
And if a boil occurs on him, then you must administer in the treatment of it the water which I mentioned before in our narration, in another place of treatment and ointment, until it is healed.
Chapter .xxxiiii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the toes.
Let him who is dislocated be bandaged with little extension of his work; and that which is not difficult, raise it lightly.
But if the dislocation is in some joints of the foot, then you must make the patient place his foot on a level ground or table, and be standing thus as if he were going.
And then you stand near his feet, and place your foot over that which protrudes from the joints, and then press it with your foot with force until it returns, and that you see it already straightened and does not appear in the place prominence.
And then place under the foot of him a table that supports the whole foot, which has two ends, and then strengthen it with a decent and firm structure for three days; and then untie it and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and its return is assured.
Chapter .xxxv.
Of the kinds of dislocations that are made with plague or with fracture or with any of them together.
When any of those causes occurs, and you are engaged in the cure of it and in its restoration, many times death occurs.
And for this reason, you must not presume over its cure, but rather he who is wise in the art, of long usage, merciful and compassionate, tardy, not bold nor presumptuous; and who uses in the beginning of medicines that cause boils to burst only so much, and leaves the patient to the divine judgment, but to him whom you hope for salvation from perdition, with the alleviation of the disease, and in which you see some hope. And therefore, strive as quickly as possible to make the reduction of it at the beginning of the cause, before it becomes a festering boil.
And therefore, if the limb returns as we desire, then administer the regimen which causes boils to burst and cures the plague with the water that convenes to it from the dried herbs.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and the bones are separated, then you must pierce the ligament of them, and do in this what we have said in the simple illnesses of this which is preceded in the places of those.
And guard and avoid according to your virtue that of the treatment in the ways of fear according to what has preceded my preaching to you; for this is better, you owe your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more salutations to your true body in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasim perfectly, of which may God be the beneficiary. Amen.
